#### Question 45 - Prediction: d ✗ (Correct: a)

**Question:**
[Question]: 6. Humans will perform tasks more reliably if:1. They understand what is required and why2. The task is challenging and complex 3. Pressure is applied which penalizes failure 4. There are incentives for quality

**LLM Response:**
```
To determine the correct answer, let’s analyze each of the four statements in the context of human performance reliability, particularly in reliability engineering and human factors:

1. **They understand what is required and why**  
This is a well-established principle in human factors and ergonomics. When individuals understand the task and its purpose (the "why"), they are more likely to perform it correctly, especially under varying conditions. Comprehension leads to better situational awareness, adaptability, and motivation. This clearly increases reliability. So, statement 1 is **correct**.

2. **The task is challenging and complex**  
Contrary to intuition, increasing challenge and complexity generally **reduces** reliability, not increases it. Complex tasks are more prone to errors, especially if not well-supported by procedures, training, or automation. In reliability engineering, simplification and standardization are used to reduce failure rates. Challenging tasks may increase engagement but also increase cognitive load and error probability. Therefore, statement 2 is **incorrect**.

3. **Pressure is applied which penalizes failure**  
High-pressure environments, especially those that punish failure, tend to increase stress, which can lead to anxiety, shortcuts, and mistakes. Fear-based motivation often reduces long-term reliability and may encourage hiding errors rather than learning from them. Just culture principles in safety management emphasize learning over blame. Thus, penalizing failure typically degrades performance reliability. Statement 3 is **incorrect**.

4. **There are incentives for quality**  
Positive reinforcement, such as incentives for doing the job correctly and reliably, improves performance. Incentives aligned with quality goals encourage attention to detail, adherence to procedures, and pride in workmanship. Unlike punitive pressure, positive incentives promote a proactive attitude toward reliability. So, statement 4 is **correct**.

Now, combining the correct statements:
- Statement 1: Correct
- Statement 4: Correct
- Statements 2 and 3: Incorrect

Therefore, the correct combination is **1 and 4**.

Final answer:

{"answer": ["d"]}
```

**Usage:**
- Tokens: 631 (209 prompt + 422 completion)
- Cost: $0.000379

#### Question 46 - Prediction: d ✗ (Correct: c)

**Question:**
[Question]: 7. Five items were placed on test, one item failed after 42 hrs, and the remaining four items were removed after 50 hrs. It is known that the time to failure for these items follows a Weibull with  $ \beta $ =2.2. Determine the 95% lower confidence limit for the characteristic life.

**LLM Response:**
```
To solve this problem, we need to determine the **95% lower confidence limit for the characteristic life** of a Weibull distribution with known shape parameter β = 2.2, based on life test data.

---

### Step 1: Understand the data

- 5 items are tested.
- 1 item failed at **42 hours**.
- 4 items were **censored at 50 hours** (i.e., they did not fail by 50 hours).
- So, this is a **life test with one failure and four right-censored items**.
- We are told the time-to-failure distribution is **Weibull with shape parameter β = 2.2** (known).
- We want the **95% lower confidence limit for the characteristic life (η)**.

> Note: In the Weibull distribution, the characteristic life η is the time at which 63.2% of the units are expected to fail. The reliability function is:
> 
> $ R(t) = \exp\left[ -\left( \frac{t}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right] $

---

### Step 2: Use the life test data to estimate η

When β is known (as here, β = 2.2), the Weibull model simplifies, and we can use a transform to the **exponential distribution** via the Weibull **log-likelihood** or **pivot methods**.

But for **censored data**, especially with only one failure, a common method is to use the **maximum likelihood estimation (MLE)** for η under known β.

For Weibull with **known β**, the MLE for η can be derived from the likelihood function:

Let’s define:

- $ t_1 = 42 $: the one failure
- $ t_2 = t_3 = t_4 = t_5 = 50 $: censored times (no failure)

The likelihood function for Weibull with shape β known is:

$$
L(\eta) = \prod_{i=1}^{n} f(t_i)^{\delta_i} \cdot R(t_i)^{1 - \delta_i}
$$

Where:
- $ \delta_i = 1 $ if failure, 0 if censored
- $ f(t) = \frac{\beta}{\eta} \left( \frac{t}{\eta} \right)^{\beta - 1} \exp\left[ -\left( \frac{t}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right] $
- $ R(t) = \exp\left[ -\left( \frac{t}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right] $

So with one failure at 42 and four censored at 50:

$$
L(\eta) = f(42) \cdot R(50)^4
= \left[ \frac{\beta}{\eta} \left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^{\beta-1} \exp\left( -\left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right) \right] \cdot \left[ \exp\left( -\left( \frac{50}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right) \right]^4
$$

$$
= \frac{\beta}{\eta} \left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^{\beta-1} \exp\left[ -\left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^\beta - 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right]
$$

Take logarithm and maximize w.r.t η.

But this can be complex. Alternatively, for **Weibull with known β**, the **MLE for η** can be found numerically.

However, for **confidence limits on η**, especially a **lower 95% confidence limit**, there is a **well-known approximate method** using the **chi-squared distribution** when β is known.

---

### Step 3: Use the pivot for m = 1 failure

There is a standard approach: when β is known, the quantity

$$
\sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \frac{t_i}{\eta} \right)^\beta
$$

is distributed as **(1/2) χ² with 2r degrees of freedom**, where r is the number of **failures**.

Wait: Actually, the pivotal quantity is:

$$
H = 2 \sum_{i=1}^{r} \left( \frac{t_i}{\eta} \right)^\beta + 2 \sum_{j=r+1}^{n} \left( \frac{t_j}{\eta} \right)^\beta \sim \chi^2_{2r}
$$

Wait — correction: For **Type II censoring (fixed number of failures)**, but here we have **Type I censoring (test ends at 50 hrs)**.

But a standard method exists:

When **β is known**, and we have a mix of failure and censoring times, then the MLE of η satisfies:

$$
\sum_{\text{failures}} \left( \frac{t_i}{\hat{\eta}} \right)^\beta + \sum_{\text{censored}} \left( \frac{t_i}{\hat{\eta}} \right)^\beta = \frac{r}{\Gamma(1 + 1/\beta)} \quad ??? Not quite.

Better approach:

Actually, the **total accumulated life** in transformed space (using $ z = t^\beta $) allows us to use:

Let:
$$
Q = 2 \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \frac{t_i}{\eta} \right)^\beta
$$
Then when β is known, $ Q \sim \chi^2_{2r} $, where r is the number of **failures**.

Wait — Actually, a better-known result:

> For a Weibull distribution with **shape β known**, the statistic
> $$
> \theta = 2 \sum_{i=1}^{r} \left( \frac{t_i}{\eta} \right)^\beta + 2 \sum_{censored} \left( \frac{t_c}{\eta} \right)^\beta
> $$
> follows a **chi-squared distribution with 2r degrees of freedom**.

Yes — this is a pivotal quantity. Refer to Nelson, "Applied Life Data Analysis".

So define:

$$
\chi^2_{\text{obs}} = 2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^\beta + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right]
$$

And this has a $ \chi^2 $ distribution with $ 2r = 2 \times 1 = 2 $ degrees of freedom.

We want a **95% lower confidence limit for η**, i.e., find $ \eta_L $ such that:

$$
P(\eta \geq \eta_L) = 0.95
$$

Equivalently, using the pivot:

We solve:

$$
2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta \right] = \chi^2_{1 - 0.95, 2} = \chi^2_{0.05, 2}
$$

Wait — **Caution**: The pivot $ Q = 2 \sum (t_i/\eta)^\beta \sim \chi^2_{2r} $

To get a **lower confidence limit** for η (i.e., we want high η to make $ t^\beta / \eta^\beta $ small), so for a **95% lower limit**, we set:

$$
2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta \right] = \chi^2_{0.05, 2}
$$

Because smaller η increases the sum — so the **lower confidence limit** for η corresponds to the **upper tail** of the chi-square? Wait — let's think carefully.

Actually, $ Q = 2 \sum (t_i / \eta)^\beta \sim \chi^2_{2r} $

For a **lower confidence bound** on η, we want:

$ P(\eta \geq \eta_L) = 0.95 $

Which implies that for smaller η, the quantity Q increases, so a small η would lead to large Q, possibly exceeding the upper quantile.

But we use:

$ $
P\left( 2 \sum \left( \frac{t_i}{\eta} \right)^\beta \leq \chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r} \right) = 1 - \alpha
$ $

So:
$$
2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta} \right)^\beta + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta} \right)^\beta \right] \leq \chi^2_{0.95, 2}
$$
gives a **lower** confidence bound on η.

Wait: Larger η ⇒ smaller (t/η) ⇒ smaller sum ⇒ smaller Q.

So to find **lower limit η_L**, we set:

$$
2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta_L} \right)^\beta \right] = \chi^2_{0.95, 2}
$$

Because we want:
P(Q ≤ χ²_{0.95}) = 0.95 ⇒ corresponds to P(η ≥ η_L) = 0.95

So yes, **solve for η_L in**:
$$
2 \left[ \left( \frac{42}{\eta_L} \right)^{2.2} + 4 \left( \frac{50}{\eta_L} \right)^{2.2} \right] = \chi^2_{0.95, 2}
$$

Look up: $ \chi^2_{0.95, 2} = 5.991 $

So:

$$
```

#### Question 35 - Prediction: a ✗ (Correct: c)

**Question:**
[Question]: 3.  10 units have entered the field in the last two months with current  times (hours): 25, 33, 40, 45, 50, 60, 64, 75, 80, 99. Failure distribution   $ \beta $ =3.02,  $ \eta $ =88 (hours) What is the expected number of units predicted to fail over the next 5 hours?

**LLM Response:**
```
To determine the expected number of units predicted to fail over the next 5 hours, we need to use the Weibull distribution, which is commonly used in reliability engineering.

Given:
- Failure distribution: Weibull with shape parameter β = 3.02 and scale parameter η = 88 hours.
- 10 units have been in service for different durations: 25, 33, 40, 45, 50, 60, 64, 75, 80, 99 hours.
- We are to find the expected number of units to fail in the next 5 hours (i.e., between current time and current time + 5 hours).

Step 1: Understand the Weibull reliability function

The cumulative distribution function (CDF) of the Weibull distribution is:

F(t) = 1 - exp[ -(t/η)^β ]

This gives the probability that a unit will fail by time t.

For a unit currently operating at time t₀, the probability it will fail in the next 5 hours (i.e., by time t₀ + 5) is:

P(failure in next 5 hrs) = F(t₀ + 5) - F(t₀)  
= [1 - exp(-( (t₀+5)/η )^β)] - [1 - exp(-( t₀/η )^β)]  
= exp(-(t₀/η)^β) - exp(-((t₀+5)/η)^β)

This is the probability that a unit with current age t₀ fails in the next 5 hours.

Step 2: Apply this to each of the 10 units

We compute for each unit’s current time t:

P_t = exp( - (t / 88)^3.02 ) - exp( - ((t + 5) / 88)^3.02 )

Then sum all 10 probabilities. The sum will be the expected number of failures in the next 5 hours (since expectation is linear, even if units are independent).

Let’s compute this step by step numerically.

We'll compute for each t:

1. t = 25  
(25/88)^3.02 ≈ (0.2841)^3.02 ≈ 0.0227  
→ exp(-0.0227) ≈ 0.9776  
(30/88)^3.02 ≈ (0.3409)^3.02 ≈ 0.0382 → exp(-0.0382) ≈ 0.9625  
P = 0.9776 - 0.9625 = 0.0151

2. t = 33  
33/88 = 0.375 → (0.375)^3.02 ≈ 0.0525 → exp(-0.0525) ≈ 0.9488  
38/88 ≈ 0.4318 → (0.4318)^3.02 ≈ 0.0795 → exp(-0.0795) ≈ 0.9236  
P = 0.9488 - 0.9236 = 0.0252

3. t = 40  
40/88 ≈ 0.4545 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.092 → exp(-0.092) ≈ 0.912  
45/88 ≈ 0.5114 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.133 → exp(-0.133) ≈ 0.8755  
P = 0.912 - 0.8755 = 0.0365

4. t = 45  
45/88 ≈ 0.5114 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.133 → exp(-0.133) ≈ 0.8755  
50/88 ≈ 0.5682 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.185 → exp(-0.185) ≈ 0.831  
P = 0.8755 - 0.831 = 0.0445

5. t = 50  
50/88 ≈ 0.5682 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.185 → exp(-0.185) ≈ 0.831

P = 0.831 - 0.780 = 0.051

6. t = 60  
60/88 ≈ 0.6818 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.338 → exp(-0.338) ≈ 0.713  
65/88 ≈ 0.7386 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.423 → exp(-0.423) ≈ 0.655  
P = 0.713 - 0.655 = 0.058

7. t = 64  
64/88 ≈ 0.7273 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.405 → exp(-0.405) ≈ 0.667  
69/88 ≈ 0.784 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.508 → exp(-0.508) ≈ 0.6015  
P = 0.667 - 0.6015 = 0.0655

8. t = 75  
75/88 ≈ 0.8523 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.625 → exp(-0.625) ≈ 0.535  
80/88 ≈ 0.909 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.755 → exp(-0.755) ≈ 0.470  
P = 0.535 - 0.470 = 0.065

9. t = 80

85/88 ≈ 0.9659 → ^3.02 ≈ 0.912 → exp(-0.912) ≈ 0.402  
P = 0.470 - 0.402 = 0.068
```

#### Question 20 - Prediction: a, b, c ✗ (Correct: a, b, c, d)

**Question:**
[Question]: 9.  In constructing programs for environmental stress screening(ESS), the  selection of the stress levels and the choice of exposure times is a  challenging task. Which of the following are true:

**LLM Response:**
```
Let’s analyze the question and each of the provided options step by step.

The question is about Environmental Stress Screening (ESS), specifically about how stress levels and exposure times are chosen, and which statements about ESS are true.

First, understand what ESS is:
- Environmental Stress Screening (ESS) is a process used primarily in manufacturing and production to expose latent defects in newly manufactured or repaired products.
- It applies controlled stresses (such as thermal cycling, vibration, etc.) to precipitate early failures (infant mortality) before products are delivered.
- It is not designed to simulate real-life environmental conditions, but rather to accelerate failure of defective units without causing wear-out of good units.

Now, evaluate each option:

[a] ESS is not a test. ESS is a screening process.
- This statement is correct. Although ESS may look like a test because it involves applying stresses, it is fundamentally a *screening process*, not a reliability *test*. A test typically gathers data to make inferences about reliability or performance. ESS, in contrast, is used to *eliminate defective units* from a batch. The goal is not to collect data for analysis, but to improve product quality by weeding out weak units. So [a] is true.

[b] ESS is not Burn-in, ESS stresses a product to operational extremes.
- This is partially correct but needs careful interpretation.
  - ESS and Burn-in are related but not identical. Burn-in typically refers to operating a product under elevated stress (often thermal and electrical) for a period to precipitate early failures. ESS often includes burn-in-like procedures but typically adds environmental stresses like vibration and thermal cycling.
  - Also, ESS does not stress a product to destructive or design limits, but typically to *environmental limits that are below the design limits but above normal operational conditions*, ensuring that only defective units fail.
  - However, it's important to note that ESS stresses are applied within operational or qualification levels, not to “extremes” that would damage good units.
  - The phrasing here is ambiguous. The distinction that “ESS is not Burn-in” is generally correct, but the claim that it stresses to “operational extremes” might be misleading. Operational extremes usually mean the upper/lower bounds of specified operation, and ESS may exceed those (e.g., thermal cycling beyond operational range but within non-damaging, qualification levels).
  - Overall, the intent of [b] is to contrast ESS with burn-in and clarify its nature. Despite minor imprecision, this statement is generally accepted as true in reliability engineering practice. So [b] can be considered true.

[c] ESS is used in manufacturing/production to catch latent failures.
- This is a core purpose of ESS. Latent failures (hidden defects from manufacturing, such as solder faults, cracked components, workmanship issues) are activated by ESS stresses. By detecting and removing such units early, field failures are reduced. This is one of the standard justifications for ESS. So [c] is clearly true.

[d] All items in a product line should be exposed to ESS.
- This is not always true. Whether 100% of units undergo ESS depends on several factors:
  - Cost and time of ESS.
  - Criticality of the product (e.g., aerospace vs. consumer electronics).
  - Historical reliability data.
  - Sampling plans may be used instead of 100% screening if the process is mature and stable.
- Standards like MIL-STD-810 or industry practices allow for sampling in some cases, especially if the manufacturing process is in control.
- Therefore, while ESS may be applied to all units in high-reliability applications (e.g., military, aerospace), it is not a universal rule that *all* items must be screened.
- So [d] is too absolute and not always true. Hence, [d] is false.

Final evaluation:
- [a] True
- [b] True
- [c] True
- [d] False

{"answer": ["a", "b", "c"]}
```

**Usage:**
- Tokens: 1087 (231 prompt + 856 completion)
- Cost: $0.000537

#### Question 7 - Prediction: d ✗ (Correct: a)

**Question:**
[Question]: 9.  A product has been produced for many years with an average yield of 85%  (That 85% is a lower 95% confidence limit). Ten batches were produced  using a new raw material with a sample average yield of 86% and a  standard deviation of 1%. At the 95% confidence level, the data indicate  the average yield is:

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given the following information:

- Historical process: Average yield has a **lower 95% confidence limit of 85%**. This implies that the long-term average yield is **at least 85% with 95% confidence**. However, this does not give us a point estimate of the population mean — just a lower bound.
- New raw material data:
  - Sample size: n = 10
  - Sample mean: x̄ = 86%
  - Sample standard deviation: s = 1%
- We are to evaluate whether the **average yield with the new raw material** is statistically different from something — but what?

Let’s interpret the question carefully:

> "At the 95% confidence level, the data indicate the average yield is:"

And the options compare the **true average yield** (population mean) to the **sample average yield of 86%**, asking whether it's greater or less, and whether it's statistically different.

Wait: That phrasing is odd. We *know* the sample average is 86%. The true population mean is unknown. The question is asking: based on the data (n=10, mean=86, s=1), what can we say about the **true average yield**?

Let’s rephrase the task:

We have a sample: n = 10, x̄ = 86%, s = 1%

We want to make a 95% confidence interval for the true average yield using the new material.

Since the population standard deviation is unknown and n is small (10), we use the **t-distribution**.

Degrees of freedom: df = 9

t-value for 95% confidence (two-sided): t* ≈ 2.262

Standard error (SE) = s / √n = 1% / √10 ≈ 1 / 3.162 ≈ 0.316%

Margin of error (ME) = t* × SE ≈ 2.262 × 0.316 ≈ 0.715%

So, 95% CI:  
86% ± 0.715% → approximately (85.285%, 86.715%)

Interpretation: We are 95% confident that the true average yield with the new material is between 85.285% and 86.715%.

Now, compare to the old process: It had a **lower 95% confidence limit of 85%** — meaning its true mean was estimated such that we are 95% confident it's ≥85%. But we don’t know the actual historical mean or variability.

But the question is not asking whether the new process is different from the old one. It's asking:

> "the data indicate the average yield is:"

And the options compare the **true average yield** to the **sample average (86%)**, and say whether it's greater/less and whether there's a statistical difference.

But — that’s conceptually flawed. The **true average yield** is not something we expect to be greater than the sample average in any deterministic sense. The sample average is our best estimate.

But statistically, from the confidence interval, we see:

- The true average yield is likely **less than** 86.715% and **greater than** 85.285%.
- The sample average is 86%, which is the point estimate.
- So, the true mean could be less than, equal to, or greater than 86% — but within the interval.

[b] greater than the sample average yield of 86% and is statistically different  
[c] greater than the sample average yield of 86% and no statistical difference  
[d] less than the sample average yield of 86% and no statistical difference

In fact, **any** hypothesis test of H₀: μ = 86% vs. H₁: μ ≠ 86% using this data would have a test statistic of:

t = (86 - 86) / (1/√10) = 0

p-value = 1.0 — so we cannot reject the null. No evidence that μ ≠ 86%.

Therefore, **statistically, there is no difference** between the sample average and itself.

df = 9

Since 3.16 > 1.833, we reject H₀: the new yield is significantly greater than 85%.

So the new average yield is greater than the historical lower bound.
```

**Question:**
[Question]: 4. Which of the following can be prevented with Preventive maintenance:

**LLM Response:**
```
Let's analyze the question step-by-step.

The question asks: "Which of the following can be prevented with preventive maintenance?"

First, understand what preventive maintenance is.

Preventive maintenance (PM) refers to regularly performed maintenance activities aimed at reducing the likelihood of equipment failure. It includes inspections, cleaning, lubrication, adjustments, and replacement of parts before they fail. The goal is to prevent unexpected breakdowns and prolong equipment life. PM is proactive and scheduled, based on time or usage.

Now, evaluate each option:

[a] Handling Damage  
Handling damage refers to physical damage that occurs during transport, installation, or日常操作 due to human error or improper use. Preventive maintenance does not directly prevent damage from mishandling. While training and procedures can reduce this risk, it is not the primary purpose of PM. So, handling damage is not preventable through preventive maintenance. → Not correct.

[b] Poor Quality Control  
Poor quality control is a systemic issue in manufacturing or processes, relating to supervision, procedures, testing, and inspection standards. It is typically addressed through quality management systems (e.g., ISO 9001), statistical process control, training, and auditing. Preventive maintenance does not fix flaws in quality control processes. → Not correct.

[c] Improper Installation  
Improper installation refers to equipment being set up incorrectly during initial setup or replacement. This is generally prevented through proper installation procedures, commissioning checklists, and skilled technicians. While preventive maintenance might detect problems *caused by* improper installation (e.g., vibration or overheating), it does not *prevent* improper installation itself. Preventing it would require correct installation protocols, not maintenance. → Not correct.

[d] Inadequate Design  
Inadequate design is a flaw in the original engineering of a system or component. It is a root cause that leads to recurring failures. Preventive maintenance cannot correct poor design. In fact, a badly designed system may still fail even with perfect maintenance. Design issues are addressed through redesign or engineering changes, not PM. → Not correct.

However, pause and reconsider: are any of these indirectly preventable through PM?

Preventive maintenance primarily prevents *equipment failures due to wear, fatigue, contamination, or degradation*—not human, design, or systemic quality errors.

But consider this: preventive maintenance includes inspections that may *detect early signs* of problems arising from these issues. For example, improper installation may cause misalignment, which PM can detect and correct. But does PM prevent improper installation from ever occurring? No—it only detects and mitigates the consequences.

Therefore, none of the listed issues are directly **prevented** by preventive maintenance in their root cause sense.

But is there a best-fit answer?

Let’s look again:

[c] Improper installation — if “improper installation” includes components being installed incorrectly during *maintenance* (e.g., replacing a part without proper torque), then a well-structured preventive maintenance program that includes proper procedures, training, and checklists might help prevent *reoccurring* improper installation *during PM events*. In that case, preventive maintenance processes themselves can include controls to prevent improper re-installation.

However, the term "improper installation" typically refers to the initial setup, not maintenance-related reassembly. So this is a stretch.

Alternatively, consider that preventive maintenance prevents **unplanned failures** due to known degradation mechanisms, but not the listed root causes.

- Handling damage → preventive through handling procedures, not PM.
- Poor quality control → addressed in manufacturing QA.
- Improper installation → avoided through proper commissioning.
- Inadequate design → fixed via design review and change.

Hence, **none** of the choices are directly preventable by preventive maintenance in the true sense.

According to standards like ISO 13372 and maintenance best practices, preventive maintenance prevents failures due to:

- Wear
- Corrosion
- Fatigue
- Aging

It does not prevent design flaws, poor installation, or human errors unless embedded in the PM process.

However, some exam questions may consider that **improper installation** during maintenance can be avoided by using standardized procedures in a PM program.

Still, the root cause is not eliminated; the prevention comes from procedure adherence, not PM per se.

Given all that, **none of the options are clearly preventable by preventive maintenance**.

But since it's a multiple-choice question and likely expects an answer, let's reconsider: is there any way one fits?

Actually, **none** of [a], [b], [c], or [d] are fundamentally prevented by preventive maintenance.

However, in some contexts, **improper installation** might be caught during preventive maintenance inspections. But "caught" is not "prevented."

Prevention means stopping it from happening.

So if a technician improperly installs a part, and PM later finds it — that’s corrective or predictive, not prevention of the installation error.

Therefore, the most accurate answer is: **none**.

But since “none” isn’t an option, and this is likely a single or multiple-select question, we must choose the best available.

Preventive maintenance is equipment-focused, time-based maintenance.

```

```
Step 1: Understand the concept of quality.
Quality generally refers to the degree to which a product meets specified requirements and customer expectations. In quality engineering, the focus is often on consistency, conformance to specifications, and minimizing defects. Key metrics include the defective rate at a given time (e.g., parts per million defective), and the concern is often with issues arising during manufacturing—such as variations, process control, and inspection.

So, choices [b] Manufacturing errors and [c] Defective rate at a point in time are more aligned with quality engineering.

Step 2: Understand the concept of reliability.
Reliability is the probability that a product, system, or component will perform its intended function under stated conditions for a specified period of time. It is inherently a time-dependent concept. The focus is on how long a product lasts before it fails and how failure rates evolve over time (e.g., early failures, useful life, wear-out phase — as in the bathtub curve).

Therefore, reliability engineering emphasizes predicting, analyzing, and mitigating failures over the lifecycle of a product. It involves tools such as failure mode and effects analysis (FMEA), reliability block diagrams, and accelerated life testing.

This makes [a] Failure rate over time strongly associated with reliability engineering.

Step 3: Consider choice [d] Product design.
While product design is important for both quality and reliability, reliability engineering has a significant overlap with design because reliability must be "designed in." However, quality also plays a role in design (e.g., Design for Six Sigma, robust design). So while design impacts both, it is not uniquely or primarily the domain of reliability over quality. Thus, [d] is less specific than [a] in differentiating reliability from quality.

Step 4: Compare all options.
The key differentiator of reliability vs. quality is the dimension of time. Quality often looks at static measures (e.g., defect rate at shipment), while reliability deals with performance over time.

Therefore, the most accurate and distinct concern of reliability engineering — compared to quality — is the failure rate over time.

Final Selection:
[a] is the best answer.

{"answer": ["a"]}
```

- Tokens: 633 (171 prompt + 462 completion)
- Cost: $0.000063

#### Question 39 - Prediction: c ✗ (Correct: b)

**Question:**
[Question]: 8.  A part for a marine engine with a constant failure rate has an MTTF of  two months. If two spare parts are carried, What is the probability of  surviving a six-month without losing the use of the engine as a result  of part exhaustion?

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given a reliability problem involving spare parts and part exhaustion over time. Let's solve it step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the scenario**

- A marine engine part has a **constant failure rate** → this implies **exponential distribution** for failure times.
- **MTTF (Mean Time To Failure)** = 2 months.
- Two **spare parts** are carried.
- We want the **probability of surviving 6 months without losing engine use due to part exhaustion**.
- That means: we start with the original part + 2 spares → **3 parts total** available.

Each time a part fails, it is replaced with a spare. After the third failure (original + 2 spares used), no spares remain. If a fourth failure happens before 6 months, we **cannot replace** the part → loss of engine use.

But wait: we want to **not lose use due to part exhaustion** during 6 months.

So we must **not exhaust the spares in 6 months**, meaning: **the third failure must occur after 6 months**.

Wait: actually — we have the **original part and 2 spares**, so **3 parts total** can be used.

So the engine can tolerate **up to 3 part failures** (the original and two spares). The **engine stops being usable only when the third spare is used and a failure occurs that cannot be replaced** → actually, **after 3 failures**, we have no more spares.

But if the **third failure occurs at or before 6 months**, then we are out of spares *during* the 6-month period → risk of engine failure *if another failure occurs*. However, the question says: “surviving a six-month without losing the use of the engine as a result of part exhaustion.”

So, **we lose use when we run out of spares before 6 months**? No, not exactly. We lose use **only when a failure occurs and we have no spare to replace it**.

Therefore, **we survive the 6 months** if the **number of failures in 6 months is ≤ 3**? No — wait:

- We have **2 spares**, so we can replace **2 failures**.
- The original part is already in use. So total usable parts = 3.

Thus, **the system fails due to part exhaustion** when the **third failure occurs** (i.e., we've used up the original and two spares — and now no more replacements).

So, **to avoid loss of engine use during 6 months**, we must **not have more than 2 failures in 6 months**? No: we can have **up to 3 parts used**, meaning **we can survive 3 failures**: the original is replaced by spare 1, then spare 2, then the third part fails and we have no more spares.

But if the **third failure happens at time t ≤ 6 months**, then from t to 6 months, we are **operating with no spares**, so **if a 4th failure occurs before 6 months**, we cannot replace — but we only care about **loss due to part exhaustion**, i.e., running out of parts.

But actually, the engine keeps running as long as parts are replaced. It **fails only when a failure occurs and there is no spare**.

Therefore, to **survive 6 months without engine failure due to part exhaustion**, we need that the **number of failures in 6 months is ≤ 3**, because:
- 1st failure: replace with spare 1
- 2nd failure: replace with spare 2
- 3rd failure: replace with last (original system + 2 spares) → now **no spares left**
- But **if a 4th failure occurs before 6 months**, then we cannot replace → engine fails.

So: **engine failure due to part exhaustion occurs only if ≥ 4 failures happen within 6 months**.

Wait — we started with the operating part and 2 spares. So:
- Total of **3 parts available for use**.

So the engine will **stop working when the 3rd spare is used**? No — when the third part fails and there is no spare, we cannot restart or continue.

But the **time of failure of the third part** is when we **run out of parts**.

So the engine continues running until the **third failure occurs**? No — it can survive three failures **only if** we have three parts.

Actually, the **engine fails due to part exhaustion** when we have a failure and **no spare available**.

Therefore:
- Failure #1: replace with spare 1 → spare count: 1 left
- Failure #2: replace with spare 2 → spare count: 0 left
- Failure #3: no spare → engine fails

So actually, we can tolerate **only 2 failures** within 6 months? No — we **start with the engine running on one part**. We replace it when it fails. So:

- The system can have **3 failures** before we are out of spares (original + 2 spares = 3 parts)

→ So the **third failure** is the last one we can replace.

After the **third failure**, we have no more spares.

But **we only lose use if the third failure happens, and then we have no ability to continue.** However, the **third failure** doesn't cause immediate stop unless a fourth is needed? No — **each failure requires a spare to keep running**.

So, if the **third part fails**, and we have no spares, we **cannot continue** → engine loses use.

Therefore, **the engine loses use due to part exhaustion at the time of the 3rd failure**, because we used up all 3 parts (original + 2 spares). That is, **after 3 failures**, we are out of parts.

Thus, to **survive 6 months without losing use**, we need that the **third failure occurs after 6 months**.

In other words: the **time to the third failure** must be **greater than 6 months**.

---

### **Step 2: Model the failure process**

- Constant failure rate → times between failures are exponential.
- So the number of failures in time t follows a **Poisson process**.
- Let λ = failure rate.

Given: MTTF = 2 months → for exponential, MTTF = 1/λ → so **λ = 1/2 = 0.5 failures per month**.

We are looking for the **probability that the third failure occurs after 6 months**.

This is the same as: **probability that the number of failures in 6 months is less than 3**, i.e., ≤ 2?

No: if the **third failure occurs after 6 months**, then **number of failures in 6 months is ≤ 2**.

Yes! Correct.

Let N(t) = number of failures in time t ~ Poisson(λt)

We want:
P(survive 6 months without part exhaustion) = P(less than 3 failures in 6 months)  
= P(N(6) ≤ 2)

Because if 0, 1, or 2 failures occur in 6 months, we **do not exhaust the 3 parts**, so we survive.

If 3 or more failures occur, then by the 3rd failure, we run out — so we **cannot survive** if 3 failures occur within 6 months.

So yes: we need P(N(6) ≤ 2)

λ = 0.5 per month  
t = 6 months  
→ λt = 0.5 × 6 = 3

So N(6) ~ Poisson(3)

P(N(6) ≤ 2) = P(0) + P(1) + P(2)

Poisson PMF: P(k) = (e^(-λt) * (λt)^k) / k!

So:
- P(0) = e^{-3} * (3^0)/0! = e^{-3} * 1 = e^{-3}
- P(1) = e^{-3} * 3^1 / 1! = 3e^{-3}
- P(2) = e^{-3} * 3^2 / 2! = e^{-3} * 9 / 2 = 4.5 e^{-3}

Sum:
P(N ≤ 2) = e^{-3} (1 + 3 + 4.5) = e^{-3} × 8.5

e^{-3} ≈ 0.049787

So:
8.5 × 0.049787 ≈ ?
```

#### Question 45 - Prediction: d ✗ (Correct: a)

**Question:**
[Question]: 6. Humans will perform tasks more reliably if:1. They understand what is required and why2. The task is challenging and complex 3. Pressure is applied which penalizes failure 4. There are incentives for quality

**LLM Response:**
```
To determine the correct answer, let's analyze each of the four statements regarding human performance and reliability in task execution:

1. **They understand what is required and why**  
This is a well-supported principle in human factors and reliability engineering. When individuals understand the task and the rationale behind it (including the consequences of failure), they are more likely to perform it reliably. Knowledge enhances both compliance and adaptability. This factor clearly improves reliability. → **Valid**

2. **The task is challenging and complex**  
While moderate challenge can engage people, high complexity increases the likelihood of errors. In reliability engineering, complexity is generally seen as a risk factor. Complex tasks are harder to perform correctly, especially under stress or time pressure. Simplicity and clarity improve reliability. Therefore, complexity does not enhance reliability. → **Invalid**

3. **Pressure is applied which penalizes failure**  
Applying pressure and penalizing failure often leads to fear-based behavior, which can reduce reliability. It may cause individuals to hide errors, rush through tasks, or experience stress-induced mistakes. A just culture that encourages reporting and learning from errors is preferred in reliability engineering. Thus, punitive pressure reduces human reliability. → **Invalid**

4. **There are incentives for quality**  
Positive incentives that reward accuracy, attention to detail, and quality of work have been shown to improve human performance. When people are motivated by recognition, rewards, or a sense of achievement, they are more likely to perform tasks reliably. Incentives aligned with quality (as opposed to just speed) support human reliability. → **Valid**

Now let's evaluate the choices:

- [a] 1 only → Includes a valid factor but excludes 4, which is also valid.
- [b] 1 & 2 → Includes valid 1 but includes invalid 2 (complexity).
- [c] 1 & 3 → Includes 1 (valid) but includes 3 (invalid—penalizing failure).
- [d] 1 & 4 → Includes both understanding (1) and incentives for quality (4), both valid.

Thus, the best choice is **[d] 1 & 4**, as it includes two factors that reliably enhance human task performance without including counterproductive elements.

{
"answer": ["d"]
}
```

**Usage:**
- Tokens: 677 (209 prompt + 468 completion)
- Cost: $0.000302

#### Question 46 - Prediction: d ✗ (Correct: c)

**Question:**
[Question]: 7. Five items were placed on test, one item failed after 42 hrs, and the remaining four items were removed after 50 hrs. It is known that the time to failure for these items follows a Weibull with  $ \beta $ =2.2. Determine the 95% lower confidence limit for the characteristic life.

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given:

- 5 items were tested.
- 1 item failed at 42 hours.
- 4 items were removed (censored) at 50 hours (no failures observed).
- Failure times follow a Weibull distribution with shape parameter β = 2.2 (known).
- We are to find the **95% lower confidence limit for the characteristic life** (also known as the scale parameter, often denoted η in the Weibull distribution).

---

### Step 1: Understanding Weibull Distribution

The Weibull distribution has two parameters:
- Shape: β (given as 2.2)
- Scale: η (characteristic life — the value we are estimating)

The **characteristic life η** is the time at which ~63.2% of units are expected to fail.

We are to find a **95% lower confidence limit** on η.

Because β is known, we can use **a single-parameter estimation method** (only η is unknown), based on failure data with Type I (time-censored) data.

---

### Step 2: Type of Censoring

This is a **mixed censoring scheme**:
- One failure at 42 hrs.
- Four units suspended (right-censored) at 50 hrs.

So, we have:
- 1 failure at t₁ = 42
- 4 suspensions at 50 (no failures)

This is **Type I censoring** (test ends at 50 hrs), but only one failure observed.

---

### Step 3: Estimation with Known β

When β is known, we can **transform Weibull data to Exponential** via the power law:

Let’s use the **Weibull log-likelihood** and apply life testing confidence bounds.

Alternatively, use a well-known method: for Weibull with known β, the quantity:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^n t_i^\beta
$$

Where $ t_i $ is the time on test for each unit (failure or censored), is **distributed as gamma** and can be related to chi-square for confidence bounds.

But note: for **suspended (censored) items**, we still include their time on test, but only up to censoring time.

However, in reliability, when β is known, we can use the fact that **the total accumulated life** on the transformed scale (raising times to power β) can be used with chi-square distribution.

Actually, for **one or more failures**, and **known β**, the **lower confidence limit on η** can be estimated using the chi-square or gamma distribution on the sum of $ t_i^\beta $.

But here, only **one failure observed**, rest are censored.

There’s a standard method:

Let:
- $ t_f = 42 $ (time to failure)
- $ t_c = 50 $ (censoring time for 4 units)
- $ r = 1 $ (number of failures)
- $ n = 5 $
- $ \beta = 2.2 $ (known)

We use the formula for the **lower confidence limit on η** when β is known and we have Type I censored data.

The method uses the fact that for Weibull:

$$
\sum_{i=1}^n (t_i/\eta)^\beta
$$

Follows a **chi-square distribution** with $ 2r $ degrees of freedom? Wait — actually, when β is known, and we sum $ t_i^\beta $ over all units (failures and suspensions), the distribution of $ 2 \sum (t_i/\eta)^\beta $ is approximately **chi-square with 2r degrees of freedom** — only for **failures**. But suspensions complicate this.

Better approach: **Transformation to Exponential**

Let $ y = t^\beta $. Then, if $ t \sim \text{Weibull}(\beta, \eta) $, then $ y \sim \text{Exponential}(\lambda = \eta^\beta) $

So, the transformed variable $ y $ has an exponential distribution with mean $ \eta^\beta $.

Therefore, we can work in the transformed domain.

Let’s compute total time on test in transformed scale.

But we only observe:
- One failure at $ y_f = 42^{2.2} $
- Four suspensions at $ y_s = 50^{2.2} $

So total accumulated "life" in transformed scale:

$$
Y_{\text{total}} = (42)^{2.2} + 4 \times (50)^{2.2}
$$

Wait — but only the failure contributes actual failure time. The suspensions contribute time without failure.

In the exponential model (in y-scale), with **Type I censoring**, and **r observed failures**, the total time on test is:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^n \min(t_i, 50)
$$

Then in y-scale: $ T_y = \sum_{i=1}^5 y_i $, where $ y_i = t_i^\beta $

So:

$$
T_y = (42)^{2.2} + 4 \times (50)^{2.2}
$$

Now, $ T_y \sim \text{Gamma}(r, \eta^\beta) $, i.e., gamma with shape = r = 1, scale = $ \eta^\beta $

But for gamma distribution with shape r, the quantity:

$$
\frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \sim \chi^2_{2r}
$$

Since r = 1:

$$
\frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \sim \chi^2_2
$$

For a **95% lower confidence limit** on η, we want:

$$
P(\eta \geq \eta_L) = 0.95
$$

From the chi-square:

$$
P\left( \frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \leq \chi^2_{0.95, 2} \right) = 0.95
$$

Wait: For lower confidence limit on η, we want to be 95% confident that $ \eta \geq \eta_L $

But $ \eta^\beta $ is in the denominator — larger $ \eta^\beta $ makes the fraction smaller.

So:

We use:

$$
\frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \leq \chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r} = \chi^2_{0.95, 2}
$$

So:

$$
\eta^\beta \geq \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{0.95, 2}}
$$

Thus, the **lower 95% CL** for $ \eta $ is:

$$
\eta_L = \left( \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{0.95, 2}} \right)^{1/\beta}
$$

Now compute numerically.

First:
- $ \beta = 2.2 $
- $ \chi^2_{0.95, 2} = 5.991 $ (since chi-square with 2 df, 95th percentile ≈ 5.991)

Now compute:
- $ 42^{2.2} = e^{2.2 \ln 42} $
  - $ \ln 42 ≈ 3.73767 $
  - $ 2.2 × 3.73767 ≈ 8.2229 $
  - $ e^{8.2229} ≈ 3725.5 $
- $ 50^{2.2} = e^{2.2 \ln 50} $
  - $ \ln 50 ≈ 3.9120 $
  - $ 2.2 × 3.9120 ≈ 8.6064 $
  - $ e^{8.6064} ≈ 5465.2 $

So,
$$
T_y = 3725.5 + 4 × 5465.2 = 3725.5 + 21,860.8 = 25,586.3
$$

Now:
$$
2 T_y = 51,172.6
$$

Divide by $ \chi^2_{0.95, 2} = 5.991 $:

$$
\frac{51,172.6}{5.991} ≈ 8,541.3
$$

Now raise to $ 1/\beta = 1/2.2 ≈ 0.454545 $:

$$
\eta_L = (8,541.3)^{0.454545}
$$

Take log:
- $ \ln(8,541.3) ≈ 9.053 $
- $ 0.454545 × 9.053 ≈ 4.115 $
- $ e^{4.115} ≈ 61.3 $

Wait — that gives η_L ≈ 61.3 hrs? But even the failure occurred at 42 hrs. That can't be — the characteristic life can’t be that high.

Wait — this is wrong. We used the **upper** chi-square percentile for a **lower CL**?

Let’s double-check the logic.

We said:

$$
\frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \sim \chi^2_{2r} = \chi^2_2
$$

We want the **lower confidence limit** on η, so we want:

$$
P(\eta \geq \eta_L) = 0.95
\Rightarrow P(\eta^\beta \geq \eta_L^\beta) = 0.95
\Rightarrow P\left( \frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \leq \frac{2 T_y}{\eta_L^\beta} \right) = 0.95
$$

So:

$$
\frac{2 T_y}{\eta_L^\beta} = \chi^2_{0.95, 2} = 5.991
\Rightarrow \eta_L^\beta = \frac{2 T_y}{5.991}
\Rightarrow \eta_L = \left( \frac{2 T_y}{5.991} \right)^{1/\beta}
$$

But wait: when $ \eta^\beta $ is small, $ \frac{2T_y}{\eta^\beta} $ is large. So to be 95% confident that $ \eta \geq \eta_L $, we need the probability that $ \frac{2 T_y}{\eta^\beta} \leq \chi^2_{0.95} $ to be 0.95, which implies:

$$
\eta_L^\beta = \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{0.95, 2r}} = \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{0.95, 2}}
$$

But this gives a **larger** denominator for smaller chi-square — let's check values.

Wait — actually, for **lower confidence limit on mean of exponential**, with r failures, we have:

$$
\eta_L^\beta = \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r}}
$$

Yes — so here, $ \alpha = 0.05 $, $ r = 1 $, so use $ \chi^2_{0.95, 2} = 5.991 $

But earlier calculation gave $ \eta_L ≈ 61.3 $ hrs, which is higher than the censoring time (50 hrs). But only one failure at 42, so characteristic life should be **less than or around 50**, but 61 seems high.

Wait — the issue: is **T_y** correctly computed?

We included **censored times raised to β** in $ T_y $. But in exponential life testing, for suspended items, we **do include their time on test**, yes.

But in the transformed Weibull-exponential analogy, **all units contribute their time on test in the y-scale**, even if suspended.

So $ T_y = \sum y_i $, with $ y_i = t_i^\beta $, $ t_i $ being failure or censoring time.

But the problem: we had **only one failure**, so the sum $ \sum y_i $ is dominated by the suspended units.

But that’s correct.

But let's consider — if we had no failures, we couldn't estimate η. But we have one failure.

However, the method assumes that the total time on test T_y, and the number of failures r=1, then:

Lower 95% CL on $ \eta^\beta $ is $ \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{0.95,2}} $

But let's plug in:

$ T_y = 42^{2.2} + 4×50^{2.2} ≈ 3725.5 + 4×5465.2 ≈ 3725.5 + 21,860.8 = 25,586.3 $

$ 2 T_y = 51,172.6 $

$ \chi^2_{0.95,2} = 5.991 $

So $ \eta_L^\beta = 51,172.6 / 5.991 ≈ 8,541.3 $

Then $ \eta_L = (8,541.3)^{1/2.2} $

Compute $ 8,541.3^{0.4545} $

As before, $ \ln(8,541.3) ≈ 9.053 $

$ 9.053 × 0.4545 ≈ 4.114 $

$ e^{4.114} ≈ 61.2 $

So η_L ≈ 61.2 hrs

But this is **greater than 50**, the test time, and failure was at 42.

$ F(t) = 1 - \exp(-(t/\eta)^\beta) $

$ \ln = 2.2 × \ln(0.817) ≈ 2.2 × (-0.202) ≈ -0.444 $

Wait — actually, there’s a **mistake**.

Wait — no: in life testing with censoring, for **exponential distribution**, the total time on test is the sum of all failure and censored times, and $ 2T / \mu \sim \chi^2_{2r} $, where r is number of failures.

Yes — so it is correct to include censored times in T.

So $ T_y = \sum y_i $, $ y_i = t_i^\beta $, and $ 2 T_y / \eta^\beta \sim \chi^2_{2r} $

$$

\Rightarrow \eta^\beta \leq \frac{2 T_y}{\chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r}}
$$

But this gives **upper limit**.

For **lower limit**, we need the **lower tail**.

Wait — this is backwards.

\quad \text{for two-sided?}
$$

No — standard formula:

For one failure, r=1, T = total time on test.

Then $ \mu_L = \frac{2T}{\chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r}} = \frac{2T}{\chi^2_{0.95,2}} $

Yes — for 95% **lower** confidence bound on mean life in exponential distribution, we use:

$$
\mu_L = \frac{2T}{\chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r}}
$$

With $ \chi^2_{1-\alpha, 2r} $, so for α=0.05, use 0.95 quantile.

$ P(\mu \geq \mu_L) = 0.95 $

From $ 2T/\mu \sim \chi^2_{2r} $, this is:
```

#### Question 23 - Prediction: a ✗ (Correct: b)

**Question:**
[Question]: 2. Given the following data: (where (-) indicates censored) -309,229,386,-104,180,-217,-167,168,122,138Determine the best fit Weibull parameters  $ \beta $ , $ \eta $ , t0?

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given a data set containing both failure times and censored data:

Data: -309, 229, 386, -104, 180, -217, -167, 168, 122, 138

Interpretation:
- Negative values (e.g., -309, -104, etc.) indicate **censored** observations.
- Positive values are **failure times**.

So extract the actual data:

Failure times (positive):  
229, 386, 180, 168, 122, 138

Censored times (absolute value of negatives):  
309, 104, 217, 167

Note: In reliability analysis, when negative values are used to denote censored data, it's a notation convention. So the censored units did **not** fail at 309, 104, etc., but were removed from test at those times.

So we have:
- Failures: 122, 138, 168, 180, 229, 386
- Censored: 104, 167, 217, 309

We need to estimate Weibull parameters β (shape), η (scale), and t₀ (location, or threshold) using this censored data.

Step 1: Consider whether t₀ = 0 or not.
- If t₀ ≠ 0, the Weibull is **3-parameter Weibull**.
- A non-zero t₀ (also called location parameter) indicates no failures occur before t₀.

Looking at failure times: the earliest failure is at 122. Censored items occurred as early as 104. So a unit was censored at 104 (before the first failure). But since it didn't fail, we can't say for sure that failure can't occur before 104.

But the first failure is at 122. So it's possible that t₀ > 0, meaning no failures possible before t₀.

Step 2: Use rank regression or maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) for censored data.

Given that we have both censored and failure data, MLE is generally preferred, especially in commercial reliability software.

But we are to pick from given choices.

We can evaluate which set of parameters best fits the data using log-likelihood intuition or compare consistency with observed failure pattern.

But since we can’t compute full MLE here manually easily, we can reason:

First, sort all events in time:

Event timeline:
104: censored (unit removed)
122: failure
138: failure
167: censored
168: failure
180: failure
217: censored
229: failure
309: censored
386: failure

So 6 failures, 4 censored.

Now, examine the choices:

[a] β = 2.76, η = 261.1, t₀ = 0 → 2-parameter Weibull  
[b] β = 0.88, η = 147.86, t₀ = 113.76 → β < 1 → decreasing failure rate  
[c] β = 2.61, η = 278.2, t₀ = 85 → similar to a, higher β  
[d] β = 1.53, η = 183, t₀ = 0.03 → almost t₀ = 0

Interpret β:
- β > 1 → increasing failure rate (IFR) → wearout behavior
- β ≈ 1 → exponential
- β < 1 → decreasing failure rate (DFR) → infant mortality

Looking at failure times:
Failures occur at: 122, 138, 168, 180, 229, 386

Time between failures:
122 → 138 (16)
138 → 168 (30)
168 → 180 (12)
180 → 229 (49)
229 → 386 (157)

Spacing is increasing — could suggest increasing reliability or wearout?

But we have censored units during this time, so failure rate trend isn't clear from spacing alone.

However, no early failures (first at 122), and units were censored earlier (at 104). But 104 is not much earlier.

But choice [b] has t₀ = 113.76 → no failures before that. First failure is 122, so that’s plausible.

But β = 0.88 < 1 — decreasing hazard. That seems inconsistent with possible wearout trend (increasing times between failures might be random, but 386 is much later).

Now, consider likelihood: if t₀ is high (like 113.76), then the effective age is t – t₀.

For failure at 122: effective time = 122 – 113.76 = 8.24

For 386: 386 – 113.76 = 272.24

But if t₀ = 113.76, then a unit censored at 104 would have t < t₀ — but that would mean the unit failed before it could fail? That’s not acceptable.

**Critical point**: In 3-parameter Weibull, all observed failure and censoring times must be > t₀.

But in choice [b], t₀ = 113.76, and we have a **censored observation at 104**, which is less than 113.76.

That is **invalid** — if t₀ = 113.76, no events (failure or censoring) can occur before t₀.

So [b] is **invalid** — reject.

Similarly, [c]: t₀ = 85
Censored at 104, 167, 217, 309, all > 85
Failures start at 122 > 85 → OK

[a] and [d] have t₀ ≈ 0 → acceptable

Now [c]: t₀ = 85, β = 2.61, η = 278.2

So effective time for Weibull: (t - t₀)

Failure at 122 → 122 - 85 = 37

But if the failure occurred at 37 units after threshold, with β = 2.61 (wearout), but the first failure is very close to t₀, then cumulative hazard is small.

But is this likely? Possible.

But can we compare likelihoods?

Alternatively, use probability plotting intuition.

With t₀ = 0, we can use median rank regression.

But we have censored data — so non-parametric rank adjustment (e.g., average order number, or Kaplan-Meier with censored) is needed.

A simple method: use Kaplan-Meier to estimate reliability at each failure, then plot on Weibull paper.

But without computation, we can reason:

First failure at 122. In presence of early censoring, the probability of failure at 122 is less than 10%-ish?

Timeline of units:

Assume 10 units start.

At 104: one censored → 9 at risk after

At 122: first failure → rank 1 of 9 → median rank ≈ 6.7%

For Weibull: F(t) = 1 - exp(-((t - t₀)/η)^β)

Try choice [a]: t₀ = 0, β = 2.76, η = 261.1

F(122) = 1 - exp(- (122/261.1)^2.76 )  
= 1 - exp(- (0.467)^2.76 )  
≈ 1 - exp(-0.467^2.76)

0.467^2.76 ≈ (0.467^2)*(0.467^0.76) ≈ 0.218 * ~0.5 (since 0.467^0.76 ≈ ?) better:

0.467^2.76 = exp(2.76 * ln(0.467)) ≈ exp(2.76 * -0.761) ≈ exp(-2.10) ≈ 0.1225

So F(122) ≈ 1 - exp(-0.1225) ≈ 1 - 0.8847 ≈ 0.115 or 11.5%

Median rank for first failure with competing risks from censoring is usually around 7-10%, so 11.5% is plausible.

Now try [c]: t₀ = 85, η = 278.2, β = 2.61

t - t₀ = 122 - 85 = 37

F(122) = 1 - exp(- (37 / (278.2 - 85))? No: η is scale for (t - t₀), so η = 278.2

So (t - t₀)/η = 37 / 278.2 ≈ 0.133

Then (0.133)^2.61 ≈ exp(2.61 * ln(0.133)) ≈ exp(2.61 * -2.017) ≈ exp(-5.26) ≈ 0.0052

Then F = 1 - exp(-0.0052) ≈ 0.0052 or 0.52%

But we have first failure at 122 — estimated unreliability should be around 10%, not 0.5%. So model [c] predicts almost no failure at 122, which contradicts data — so [c] is **unrealistic**.

Now [d]: β = 1.53, η = 183, t₀ = 0.03 ≈ 0

F(122) = 1 - exp(- (122/183)^1.53 )  
122/183 ≈ 0.6667  
0.6667^1.53 ≈ exp(1.53 * ln(0.6667)) ≈ exp(1.53 * -0.4055) ≈ exp(-0.620) ≈ 0.538  
F = 1 - exp(-0.538) ≈ 1 - 0.584 ≈ 0.416 → 41.6%

Too high — first failure should not have 41.6% unreliability — we expect less than 20% — so [d] overestimates early failure.

Now [a]: we had F(122) ≈ 11.5% — reasonable.

Last failure at 386

[a]: t = 386, t/η = 386 / 261.1 ≈ 1.478  
(1.478)^2.76 ≈ exp(2.76 * ln(1.478)) ≈ exp(2.76 * 0.391) ≈ exp(1.079) ≈ 2.94  
F = 1 - exp(-2.94) ≈ 1 - 0.0527 ≈ 0.947 → 94.7%

With 6 failures out of 10, 60% failed — but survival function should be ~40% at end. But 94.7% failure probability at 386 seems high? Wait — F(386) = 94.7% means 94.7% of units failed by 386 — but only 6/10 did. But with censored, the actual fraction failed is 60%, so unreliability should be less.

But parametric model can extrapolate.

But with t₀ = 0, β > 1, η around 260, median life is η*(ln2)^{1/β} ≈ 261.1 * (0.693)^{1/2.76} ≈ 261.1 * (0.693)^0.362 ≈ 261.1 * 0.87 ≈ 227

So median failure at ~227 — but we have one failure at 386, which is way above — but with β > 1, tail might be thin — wait, 386 is 1.5×η — but with β = 2.76, probability above η is exp(-1^β) = exp(-1) ≈ 37% — so 37% survive beyond η = 261.1 — so 386 is possible.

But 6 units failed, 4 censored. The largest failure is 386, largest censored is 309.

At t=309 (censored), with [a]: F(309) = 1 - exp(- (309/261.1)^2.76 ) ≈ 1 - exp(- (1.183)^2.76 )  
1.183^2.76 ≈ exp(2.76 * ln(1.183)) ≈ exp(2.76 * 0.167) ≈ exp(0.461) ≈ 1.586

So 79.5% failure probability by 309 — so likelihood that a unit censored at 309 is a failure survivor is only 20.5%, plausible.
```

#### Question 35 - Prediction: a ✗ (Correct: c)

**Question:**
[Question]: 3.  10 units have entered the field in the last two months with current  times (hours): 25, 33, 40, 45, 50, 60, 64, 75, 80, 99. Failure distribution   $ \beta $ =3.02,  $ \eta $ =88 (hours) What is the expected number of units predicted to fail over the next 5 hours?

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given:

- 10 units have been in the field for varying times (current ages):  
  25, 33, 40, 45, 50, 60, 64, 75, 80, 99 hours  
- The failure time distribution is Weibull with parameters:  
  Shape β = 3.02, Scale η = 88 hours  
- We are asked: **What is the expected number of units predicted to fail over the next 5 hours?**

---

### Step 1: Understand the Weibull Distribution

The Weibull reliability function is:

$$
R(t) = \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right)
$$

The cumulative distribution function (CDF), which gives the probability that a unit fails by time $ t $, is:

$$
F(t) = 1 - \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right)
$$

We are not starting from time 0 — each unit already has a current age.

So, to find the probability that a unit fails in the **next 5 hours**, given it has survived to its current age $ t $, we use the **conditional probability**:

$$
P(\text{fail in next 5 hours} \mid \text{survived to } t) = \frac{F(t+5) - F(t)}{R(t)} = 1 - \frac{R(t+5)}{R(t)}
$$

Alternatively:

$$
P_{\text{failure in next 5 hrs}} = 1 - \frac{R(t+5)}{R(t)} = 1 - \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t+5}{\eta}\right)^\beta + \left(\frac{t}{\eta}\right)^\beta \right)
$$

But numerically, it's easier to compute:

$$
R(t) = \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right)
$$

$$
R(t+5) = \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t+5}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right)
$$

Then:

$$
P(\text{fail between } t \text{ and } t+5) = R(t) - R(t+5)
$$

Wait — no: that’s not correct. **R(t)** is the probability of surviving *beyond* time t.

So the probability of failing between t and t+5 is:

$$
P(t < T \leq t+5) = F(t+5) - F(t) = [1 - R(t+5)] - [1 - R(t)] = R(t) - R(t+5)
$$

Yes — correct. So:

$$
P(\text{fail in next 5 hours} \mid \text{current age } t) = R(t) - R(t+5)
$$

Wait — no, that would be if R(t) is P(survive beyond t), then:

- P(fail by t+5) = 1 - R(t+5)  
- P(fail by t) = 1 - R(t)  
- So P(fail between t and t+5) = [1 - R(t+5)] - [1 - R(t)] = R(t) - R(t+5)

✅ Correct.

So for each unit with current age $ t_i $, compute:

$$
P_i = R(t_i) - R(t_i + 5) = \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t_i}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right) - \exp\left(-\left(\frac{t_i + 5}{\eta}\right)^\beta\right)
$$

Then sum all $ P_i $ over the 10 units — this sum is the **expected number of failures** in the next 5 hours.

---

### Step 2: Compute for each unit

Given:  
β = 3.02, η = 88

We’ll compute for each $ t_i $:

Let’s compute $ R(t) = \exp\left(-(t/88)^{3.02}\right) $

We will calculate $ R(t_i) $ and $ R(t_i + 5) $, then difference.

Let’s make a table.

| t_i | t_i/88 | (t_i/88)^3.02 | R(t_i) | t_i+5 | (t_i+5)/88 | ((t_i+5)/88)^3.02 | R(t_i+5) | P_i = R(t_i) - R(t_i+5) |
|-----|--------|----------------|--------|--------|-------------|---------------------|----------|----------------------------|
| 25 | 0.2841 | (0.2841)^3.02 ≈ 0.0223 | e^(-0.0223) ≈ 0.9779 | 30 | 0.3409 | (0.3409)^3.02 ≈ 0.0385 | e^(-0.0385) ≈ 0.9622 | 0.9779 - 0.9622 = 0.0157 |
| 33 | 0.3750 | 0.375^3.02 ≈ 0.0596 | e^(-0.0596) ≈ 0.9421 | 38 | 0.4318 | 0.4318^3.02 ≈ 0.0950 | e^(-0.0950) ≈ 0.9094 | 0.9421 - 0.9094 = 0.0327 |
| 40 | 0.4545 | 0.4545^3.02 ≈ 0.1180 | e^(-0.1180) ≈ 0.8887 | 45 | 0.5114 | 0.5114^3.02 ≈ 0.1750 | e^(-0.1750) ≈ 0.8393 | 0.8887 - 0.8393 = 0.0494 |
| 45 | 0.5114 | 0.1750 | e^(-0.1750) ≈ 0.8393 | 50 | 0.5682 | 0.5682^3.02 ≈ 0.2500 | e^(-0.2500) ≈ 0.7788 | 0.8393 - 0.7788 = 0.0605 |
| 50 | 0.5682 | 0.2500 | 0.7788 | 55 | 0.6250 | 0.625^3.02 ≈ 0.3450 | e^(-0.3450) ≈ 0.7082 | 0.7788 - 0.7082 = 0.0706 |
| 60 | 0.6818 | 0.6818^3.02 ≈ 0.416? Wait, better compute more carefully.

Let’s use calculator-style approximations.

We can use:

Let’s compute $ x = (t / 88)^{3.02} $

Since doing manual exponents is error-prone, let's use approximate values.

Alternatively, use Python-style logic.

But since this is exam-style, let's use consistent approx.

P = 0.9779 - 0.9619 = 0.0160
```

#### Question 25 - Prediction: c ✗ (Correct: a)

**Question:**
[Question]: 5.  You are developing a new product. To effectively market this product  the failure rate of your product must be 1/1000 hours or less. In  testing to date, you have experienced failures at 30 hours, 600 hours,  1400 hours and 2500 hours. How much more testing (and fixing) is  required?

**LLM Response:**
```
We are given a reliability goal and some failure data, and we need to estimate how much more testing (and fixing) is required so that the product meets the failure rate requirement of ≤1 failure per 1000 hours.

### Step 1: Understand the requirement
The requirement is: **failure rate ≤ 1 per 1000 hours**, which means:
- Failure rate λ ≤ 0.001 failures/hour

This is equivalent to requiring a **Mean Time Between Failures (MTBF)** of at least 1000 hours, because:
- MTBF = 1 / λ → so if λ ≤ 0.001, then MTBF ≥ 1000 hours.

But note: this is a **goal**, and we are in a development phase. We've observed 4 failures so far at:
- 30 hrs, 600 hrs, 1400 hrs, 2500 hrs

We assume these are **failure times** in **successive testing**, possibly including fixes (i.e., after each failure, a fix may have been made). This suggests we are in a **reliability growth** scenario.

### Step 2: Identify the model — Reliability Growth Model (Duane Model)
When failures are found and corrected during testing, we often use a **reliability growth model**. The **Duane model** is commonly used, which assumes that MTBF increases linearly with cumulative test time on a log-log plot.

But before jumping into complex modeling, let’s see what we can derive simply.

### Step 3: Total test time and failure rate so far
We have failures at:
- 30, 600, 1400, 2500 hours → Total test time = 2500 hours
- Number of failures = 4

So the **observed failure rate** = 4 / 2500 = 0.0016 failures/hour → which is **worse** than the required 0.001.

We are not yet meeting the target.

If no reliability growth occurred (i.e., constant failure rate), we could project:
- To achieve λ ≤ 0.001, we would need MTBF ≥ 1000
- But with constant failure process (homogeneous Poisson), we’d expect continued rate of 0.0016, which is too high.

But the key is: **the failure times are increasing in intervals** — suggesting improvement.

Let’s look at the **inter-failure times**:
- 0 → 30: 30 hrs
- 30 → 600: 570 hrs
- 600 → 1400: 800 hrs
- 1400 → 2500: 1100 hrs

So the inter-failure times are: **30, 570, 800, 1100**

This shows a clear **increasing trend** — the time between failures is increasing. That suggests **reliability is growing**.

### Step 4: Fit a simple reliability growth trend
Let’s assign cumulative test time and cumulative failures:

| Cumulative Time | Cumulative Failures |
|-----------------|---------------------|
| 30              | 1                   |
| 600             | 2                   |
| 1400            | 3                   |
| 2500            | 4                   |

We can plot cumulative failures vs. time. In reliability growth, we often plot **cumulative MTBF** or use Duane plots (cumulative MTBF vs. cumulative time on log scale).

But for estimation, we can assume that reliability continues to grow, and look at the inter-failure intervals.

The inter-failure times: 30, 570, 800, 1100

We want the **MTBF to reach at least 1000 hours** and **stabilize** there.

Let’s look at the trend in inter-failure times:
- 30 → 570 → 800 → 1100
- Differences: +540, +230, +300 — not perfectly linear, but increasing.

Fitting a rough linear trend to inter-failure times:
Let’s assume the improvement is such that each inter-failure interval increases.

We can assume that to meet the requirement, the **MTBF must be at least 1000**, and we need to demonstrate it.

In reliability growth, a common rule is to continue testing until the **current inter-failure time exceeds the target MTBF** several times.

Here, the **last inter-failure time is 1100 hours**, which is **above 1000**.

But we only have one interval >1000. Typically, to **demonstrate** reliability, we need several such intervals or a statistical demonstration.

But the requirement is not just one interval — it’s a **failure rate over time**, so we must have sufficient confidence that the **long-term failure rate is ≤0.001**.

In reliability growth testing (e.g., using Duane model), testing continues until the **estimated current MTBF ≥ target**, and sufficient data is gathered.

A common approach: testing continues until **cumulative test time is about 3–5 times the target MTBF**, and failure rate trends down.

But here, since we are showing growth, and the last interval was 1100 > 1000, we may need **more time to demonstrate sustained reliability**.

Let’s consider a rule of thumb: to **demonstrate** MTBF of 1000 hours with high confidence, you often need **about 3 to 5 times the target MTBF in test time** — but that’s for constant failure rate.

However, in growth scenarios, we can use **projected MTBF**.

Alternatively, note that the **required MTBF is 1000**, and we just reached an inter-failure interval of 1100 — that’s encouraging.

But with only 4 failures, and the first intervals much smaller, the **average** is still low.

We need the **failure rate going forward** to be ≤ 1/1000.

Assume that reliability continues to grow. But even if it stabilizes at 1100–1200 hours MTBF, we still need to **verify** it.

But the question is: **how much more testing (and fixing) is required?**

The last failure was at 2500 hours. Suppose we continue testing.

We want to confirm that MTBF ≥ 1000.

A common test is: if we test for **T additional hours** with **no failures**, the demonstrated MTBF (with 60% confidence, for example) would be around T / 0.8 (approx).

To demonstrate MTBF ≥ 1000 with, say, 50–60% confidence, we could use the **no-failure test**: test for 1000 * ln(1/(1-C)) / ln(e) → simpler: for 63% confidence, zero failures in 1000 hours demonstrates MTBF ≈ 1000.
```
